Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Qisas for Mudiha: Ruling and Quranic Evidence
Ruling on qisas for mudiha with Quranic proof and rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qisas is wajib for an intentional mudiha and explain the Quranic evidence from Surah al-Ma'idah 5:45.

Core Idea
Qisas for mudiha is wajib because the knife reaches the bone and death is not feared, per the Quran.

This section concludes the discussion of head wounds by addressing the ruling on mudiha (مُوضِحَة). The passage clearly states that qisas (retaliation) is obligatory for an intentional mudiha. The proof is the Quranic verse "Wal-juruḥu qiṣāṣ" (And wounds are subject to retaliation). This ruling is based on the nature of the wound: it exposes the bone but death is not typically feared, so qisas applies instead of blood money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage states: 'fī al-muḍiḥati al-qiṣāṣ' — for the mudiha, qisas is obligatory. The condition is that the wound is intentional ('amd).
The passage says 'fa-in kānat hādhihi al-shijāj... fī al-muḍiḥati al-qiṣāṣ' implying that if these wounds are intentional, then for mudiha qisas.
2
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Why qisas? Because 'al-sikkīn yantahī ilā al-‘aẓm wa lā yukhāfu minhu al-halāk ghāliban' – the knife reaches the bone and death is not usually feared. Thus, it is not a fatal wound, so qisas is possible without causing death.
This distinguishes mudiha from deeper wounds like ammah or damigha where qisas would kill.
3
Cite the Quranic evidence
The verse 'Wal-juruḥu qiṣāṣ' (Surah al-Ma'idah, 5:45) is the direct dalil. The imperative form indicates obligation (wujub). The generic word 'juruḥ' covers mudiha.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufīdu al-wujūb.

Source Text

كتاب الديات
هي : العين، سميت بها لأن الدم يخرج منها بقدر الدمع من القلة وقيل لأن عينيه تدمع بسبب ألم يحصل له منها. وفي المحيط الدامعة التي يخرج منها ما يشبه الدمع مأخوذة من دمع والدامية وهي التي يسيل منها الدم. وذكر المرغيناني أن الدامية هي التي تدمي من غير أن يسيل منها دم هو الصحيح يروى عن أبي عبيد والدامعة وهي التي يسيل منها الدم كدمع العين. ومن قال أن صاحبها تدمع عيناه من الألم فقد أبعد والباضعة وهي التي تبضع الجلد أي تقطعه مأخوذة من البضع وهو الشق والقطع ومنه مبضع الفصاد. أقول: في تفسير الباضعة بما ذكره الشارح فتور وإن تابعه صاحب الكافي وكثير من المتأخرين فيه لأن قطع الجلد متحقق في الصورة الأولى منها لا سيما في الدامعة والدامية إذا الظاهر أن شيئاً من إظهار الدم وأصالته لا يتصور بدون قطع الجلد وقد صرح الشراح بتحقق قطع الجلد في كل الأنواع العشرة للشجة فكان التفسير المذكور شاملاً للكل غير مختص بالباضعة، فالظاهر في تفسير الباضعة هو ما ذكر في المحيط والبدائع حيث قال في المحيط ثم الباضعة وهي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه. وقال في البدائع والباضعة هي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه ا هـ. ويعضد ذلك ما وقع في معتبرات كتب اللغة قال في المغرب وفي الشجاج الباضعة وهي التي جرحت الجلد وشقت اللحم ا هـ . وقال في الصحاح : الباضعة الشجة التي تقطع الجلد وتشق اللحم وتدمي إلا أنها لا تسيل الدم. وقال في القاموس والباضعة الشجة التي تقطع الجلد وتشق اللحم شقاً خفيفاً وتدمي إلا أنها لا تسيل الدم ا هـ. لا يقال فعلى هذا يلزم تشبيه الباضعة بالمتلاحمة فإنهم قالوا والمتلاحمة هي التي تأخذ في اللحم وهذا في المآل غير ما نقلته عن المحيط والبدائع في تفسير الباضعة لأنا نقول : من فسر الباضعة بما لا يقول بتفسير المتلاحمة بما ذكر حتى يلزم الاشتباه بل يزيد عليه قيداً، وعن هذا قال في المحيط : ثم الباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه قال شيخ الإسلام : ولا تنزع شيئاً من اللحم . ثم المتلاحمة وهي التي تقطع اللحم وتنزع شيئاً من اللحم؛ إلى هنا لفظ المحيط . وقال في البدائع : والباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم أي تقطعه، والمتلاحمة التي تذهب في اللحم أكثر مما تذهب الباضعة فيه. وقال في المغرب والمتلاحمة من الشجاج هي التي تشق اللحم دون العظم ثم تتلاحم بعد شقها أي تتلاءم ا.هـ. وقال في الصحاح : والمتلاحمة الشجة التي أخذت في اللحم دون العظم ثم تتلاحم ولم تبلغ السمحاق ا هـ . وقال في القاموس : وشجة متلاحمة أخذت فيه ولم تبلغ السمحاق والمتلاحمة وهي التي تأخذ في اللحم كله ثم تتلاحم بعد ذلك أي تلتئم وتتلاصق سميت بذلك تفاؤلاً على ما يؤول إليه. وروي عن محمد أن المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة لأن المتلاحمة من قولهم التحم الشيئان إذا اتصل أحدهما بالآخر التي تظهر اللحم ولا تقطعه والباضعة بعدها لأنها تقطعه. وفي ظاهر الرواية والمتلاحمة تعمل في قطع أكثر اللحم وهي بعد الباضعة . وقال الأزهري : الأوجه أن يقال فالمتلاحمة هي قلنا من المعنى الظاهر هي
المتلاحمة أي القاطعة للحم والاختلاف الذي وجد في الشجاج راجع إلى مأخذ الاشتقاق لا إلى الحكم والسمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى السمحاق وهي الجلدة الرقيقة التي بين اللحم وعظم الرأس، والموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم أي تبينه، والهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، والمنقلة وهي التي تنقل العظم بعد الكسر أي تحوله والآمة وهي التي تصل إلى أم الدماغ، وأم الدماغ هي الجلدة الرقيقة التي تجمع الدماغ، وبعد الآمة شجة تسمى الدامغة بالغبن المعجمة وهي التي تصل إلى الدماغ لم يذكرها محمد لأن النفس لا تبقى بعدها عادة فتكون قتلاً، ولا تكون من الشجاج والكلام في الشجاج ولذا لم يذكر الخارصة والدامغة لأنها لا يبقى لها في الغالب أثر . وهذه الشجاج تختص بالرأس والوجه وما كان في غيرهما يسمى جراحة فهذا هو الحقيقة والحكم يترتب على الحقيقة فلا يجب بالجراحة ما يجب بالشجة من المقدار لأن التقدير بالنقل وهو إنما ورد في الشجاع وهي تختص بالرأس والوجه فخص الحكم المقدم بها، ولا يجوز إلحاق الجراحة بها دلالة ولا قياساً لأنها ليست في معناها في الشين لأن الوجه والرأس يظهران في الغالب وغيرهما مستور غالباً لا يظهر. واختلفوا في اللحيين فعندهما في الوجه فيتحقق الشجاج فيهما فيجب فيها موجبها خلافاً لما يقول مالك رحمه الله فإنه يقول إنهما ليسا من الوجه لأن المواجهة لا تقع بهما، ونحن نقول هما متصلان بالوجه من غير فاصل ويتحقق معنى المواجهة فصار كالذقن لأنهما تحتها. وقال شيخ الإسلام ويجب أن يفرض غسلهما في الوضوء لأنهما من الوجه حقيقة إلا أنا تركناهما للإجماع ولا إجماع هنا فبقينا العيرة للحقيقة .
وفي المبسوط : الشجاج في الرأس والوجه أحد عشر أولها الخارصة وهي تشق الجلد مأخوذة من قولهم خرص القصار الثوب إذا شقه من الدق ثم الدامعة وهي التي يخرج منها ما يشبه الدمع مأخوذة من دمع العين. ولم يذكرها محمد لأنها لم يبق لها أثر في الغالب، ثم التي يخرج منها الدم ثم الباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم، ثم المتلاحمة، وعن محمد أنه جعل المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة خلافاً لأبي يوسف وتفسيرها عند أبي يوسف التي تقشر الجلد وتجمع اللحم في موضع الجراحة ولا تقطعه مأخوذة من التحام؛ يقال التحم الجيشان إذا اجتمعا . ثم السمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى جلدة رقيقة فوق العظم تسمى السمحاق، ثم الموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم واللحم، ثم الهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، ثم المنقلة التي يخرج منها العظم لأنها تكسر العظم وتنقله عن موضعه، ثم الآمة التي تصل إلى أم الرأس وهي الجلدة التي فوق الدماغ، ثم الدامغة التي تخرق الجلد وتصل إلى الدماغ ولم يذكرها محمد لأن الإنسان لا يعيش معها. وأما أحكامها فإن كانت هذه الشجاج . ففي الموضحة القصاص لأن السكين ينتهي إلى العظم ولا يخاف منه الهلاك غالباً فيجب القصاص لقوله تعالى والجروح قصاص [المائدة : ٥] وذكر الكرخي عنه أنه ليس في شيء من عمداً
Book of Blood Money. It is: the eye, named so because blood flows from it like tears due to sparseness, or because one's eyes tear due to pain. In al-Muhit: ad-dami‘a is that from which something resembling tears flows, taken from tears; ad-damiya is that from which blood flows. Al-Marghinani mentioned that ad-damiya is that which bleeds without blood flowing—that is correct, related from Abu ‘Ubayd; ad-dami‘a is that from which blood flows like tears. Whoever says that the person's eyes tear from pain has gone far. Al-badi‘a is that which splits the skin, i.e., cuts it, taken from al-bad‘ meaning splitting and cutting, from which is the lancet. I say: the interpretation of al-badi‘a as mentioned by the commentator is weak, even if followed by the author of al-Kafi and many later scholars, because cutting the skin is realized in the first form, especially in ad-dami‘a and ad-damiya, since it is apparent that nothing of blood manifestation and essence is conceivable without cutting the skin. The commentators have explicitly stated the realization of cutting the skin in all ten types of head wound. So the mentioned interpretation covers all, not specific to al-badi‘a. The apparent interpretation of al-badi‘a is what is mentioned in al-Muhit and al-Bada’i‘: in al-Muhit, 'then al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it.' In al-Bada’i‘: 'al-badi‘a is that which cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it.' This is supported by reliable lexicons: in al-Mughrib: 'among head wounds, al-badi‘a is that which wounds the skin and splits the flesh.' In as-Sihah: 'al-badi‘a is the head wound that cuts the skin and splits the flesh and bleeds but does not cause blood to flow.' In al-Qamus: 'al-badi‘a is the head wound that cuts the skin and splits the flesh slightly and bleeds but does not cause blood to flow.' It cannot be said that this causes al-badi‘a to resemble al-mutalahima, because they said al-mutalahima is that which penetrates the flesh. This is ultimately not what I transmitted from al-Muhit and al-Bada’i‘ regarding the interpretation of al-badi‘a, for we say: the one who interprets al-badi‘a as such does not interpret al-mutalahima as mentioned such that confusion arises; rather, he adds a condition. For this, al-Muhit said: 'then al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it. Shaykh al-Islam said: it does not remove any flesh. Then al-mutalahima: it cuts the flesh and removes some flesh.' Up to here is the wording of al-Muhit. Al-Bada’i‘ said: 'al-badi‘a: it cuts the flesh, i.e., cuts it. Al-mutalahima: it goes into the flesh more than al-badi‘a does.' Al-Mughrib said: 'al-mutalahima among head wounds is that which splits the flesh without [reaching] the bone, then heals after splitting, i.e., becomes coherent.' As-Sihah said: 'al-mutalahima is the head wound that penetrates the flesh without the bone, then heals and does not reach as-simhaq.' Al-Qamus said: 'a mutalahima head wound: it penetrated the flesh and did not reach as-simhaq. Al-mutalahima is that which penetrates all the flesh then heals afterwards, i.e., closes and adheres; named so optimistically for its outcome.' It is related from Muhammad that al-mutalahima comes before al-badi‘a because al-mutalahima is from their saying 'the two things joined' when one connects with the other, which shows the flesh and does not cut it; al-badi‘a comes after because it cuts it. In the apparent narration (Zahir ar-Riwaya), al-mutalahima works in cutting more flesh and comes after al-badi‘a. Al-Azhari said: the most appropriate is to say al-mutalahima is, we say from the apparent meaning, it is the cutter of flesh. The difference found in head wounds refers to the derivation, not to the ruling. As-simhaq is that which reaches as-simhaq, which is the thin membrane between the flesh and the skull. Al-mudiha is that which exposes the bone, i.e., makes it apparent. Al-hashima is that which crushes the bone. Al-munaqqila is that which moves the bone after breakage, i.e., displaces it. Al-ammah is that which reaches the mother of the brain, and the mother of the brain is the thin membrane that contains the brain. After al-ammah there is a wound called ad-damigha (with a dotted ghayn) which reaches the brain; Muhammad did not mention it because life usually does not remain after it, so it is killing, and it is not among head wounds; the discussion is about head wounds. For this reason, he did not mention al-kharisa and ad-damigha because they usually leave no mark. These head wounds are specific to the head and face; what is elsewhere is called a wound (jarahah). This is the reality, and the ruling is based on reality. So what is obligatory for a head wound is not obligatory for a wound in terms of amount, because the determination is by transmitted texts, and that was only reported for head wounds, which are specific to the head and face, so the ruling is specific to them. It is not permissible to analogize wounds to them by implication or qiyas, because they are not similar in shamefulness, since the face and head are usually apparent, while others are usually covered, not apparent. They differed regarding the two jaws (al-lahyayn). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they are part of the face, so head wounds are realized on them, and their due is obligatory. Contrary to what Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) says, he says they are not part of the face because facing does not occur with them. We say: they are connected to the face without a separator and the meaning of facing is realized, so they become like the chin because they are below it. Shaykh al-Islam said: it would be obligatory to prescribe washing them in wudu' because they are actually part of the face, except we omitted it due to consensus; but here there is no consensus, so we adhere to the reality. In al-Mabsut: head wounds on the head and face are eleven. First: al-kharisa, which splits the skin, taken from their saying 'the fuller split the cloth when beating it.' Then ad-dami‘a, from which something resembling tears flows, taken from tears of the eye; Muhammad did not mention it because it usually leaves no effect. Then that from which blood flows. Then al-badi‘a, which cuts the flesh. Then al-mutalahima. From Muhammad that he placed al-mutalahima before al-badi‘a, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Its interpretation according to Abu Yusuf is that it peels the skin and collects the flesh at the site of the wound without cutting it, taken from iltiham; it is said 'the two armies joined' when they gathered. Then as-simhaq, which reaches a thin membrane above the bone called as-simhaq. Then al-mudiha, which exposes the bone and flesh. Then al-hashima, which crushes the bone. Then al-munaqqila, from which bone comes out, because it breaks the bone and moves it from its place. Then al-ammah, which reaches the mother of the head, which is the membrane above the brain. Then ad-damigha, which pierces the skin and reaches the brain; Muhammad did not mention it because a person does not live with it. As for their rulings: if these head wounds occur intentionally, then for al-mudiha there is qisas because the knife reaches the bone and death is not usually feared, so qisas is obligatory due to His statement: 'And wounds are subject to retaliation' (al-Ma'idah 5:45). Al-Karkhi mentioned from him that there is not in anything of intentional...

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the types of head wounds (shijaj) and their rulings. The author first defines various terms for wounds on the head and face, giving linguistic derivations and quoting earlier authorities like al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', and lexicons. He distinguishes between dami'a (blood flows like tears) and damiya (blood appears but does not flow), and explains the difference between badi'a (cuts flesh without removing any) and mutalahima (cuts and removes some flesh). He notes a difference of opinion between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the order of these two, with Imam Muhammad placing mutalahima before badi'a, but the Zahir al-Riwayah placing it after. The commentary clarifies that these differences are linguistic and do not affect the legal ruling. Next, the passage defines simhaq (reaches the membrane above bone), mudiha (exposes bone), hashima (crushes bone), munaqqila (displaces bone), ammah (reaches the brain membrane), and damigha (penetrates to brain, usually fatal). It emphasizes that head wounds (shijaj) are specific to the head and face; wounds elsewhere are called jarahah and are not subject to the fixed amounts set for shijaj. The author then discusses the inclusion of the jaws (lahyayn): according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, they are part of the face and thus shijaj apply, contrary to Malik. Finally, the passage states the ruling on qisas for mudiha: retaliation is obligatory because the knife reaches the bone and death is not typically feared, citing the Quranic verse 'and wounds are subject to retaliation'. The mention of al-Karkhi suggests a transmission that qisas is not applicable in some intentional cases, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind, exacting the same injury on the offender.
الموضحة (al-muḍiḥah): A head wound that exposes the bone, making it apparent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the wujub of qisas for an intentional mudiha?
A) Consensus of the Companions
B) The Quranic verse 'Wal-juruḥu qiṣāṣ'
C) A hadith from the Prophet
D) Qiyas on murder

Key Takeaway
Qisas for an intentional mudiha is wajib because the knife reaches the bone without causing likely death, as supported by the Quranic command 'Wal-juruḥu qiṣāṣ'.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Definition and Specificity of Head Wounds (Shijaj)
Defining shijaj and distinguishing them from jirahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shijaj, list its types, explain why it is limited to head/face, and state the Hanafi position on the jaws.

Core Idea
Shijaj are wounds specific to head/face with fixed diyat; other wounds are jirahah with no fixed amounts.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author delineates the precise definitions of various head wound types (shijaj) and establishes their legal specificity. Understanding this distinction is crucial because the diyat (blood money) amounts prescribed in hadith apply only to shijaj, not to general wounds (jirahah). The passage also addresses a notable intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether the jaws (lahyayni) are included in the face for these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of shijaj described
The author lists seven types: mutalahimah (cutting flesh), samhaq (reaching the thin membrane), mawdihah (exposing bone), hashimah (breaking bone), munaqqilah (displacing bone), ammah (reaching the brain's membrane), and damighah (reaching the brain). Note that damighah is omitted from standard shijaj because it is lethal.
Only six types are usually counted; the author explains why some are omitted.
2
Understand the criterion for omission
Imam Muhammad omitted damighah because it is almost always fatal, thus it falls under homicide (qatl), not shijaj. Similarly, al-kharisah and damighah are omitted because they leave no trace.
The discussion is limited to non-lethal wounds.
3
Analyze the specificity rule
Shijaj are limited to the head and face. This is based on hadith transmission (al-taqdir bi al-naql) and the fact that the head/face are normally exposed, causing more embarrassment. Injuries elsewhere are called jirahah and have no fixed diyat.
Analogy (qiyas) to jirahah is impermissible because of the different nature of the areas.
4
Examine the dispute about the jaws (lahyayni)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the jaws part of the face, so shijaj rulings apply. Imam Malik excludes them. The Hanafi evidence: the jaws are connected to the face without a separator and are beneath the chin, which is definitely part of the face.
The author supports inclusion using the example of wudu.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that jaws are included in the face for shijaj rulings. Therefore, wounds on the jaws receive fixed diyat according to their type.
This is the position adopted for fatwa.

المتلاحمة أي القاطعة للحم والاختلاف الذي وجد في الشجاج راجع إلى مأخذ الاشتقاق لا إلى الحكم والسمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى السمحاق وهي الجلدة الرقيقة التي بين اللحم وعظم الرأس، والموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم أي تبينه، والهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، والمنقلة وهي التي تنقل العظم بعد الكسر أي تحوله والآمة وهي التي تصل إلى أم الدماغ، وأم الدماغ هي الجلدة الرقيقة التي تجمع الدماغ، وبعد الآمة شجة تسمى الدامغة بالغبن المعجمة وهي التي تصل إلى الدماغ لم يذكرها محمد لأن النفس لا تبقى بعدها عادة فتكون قتلاً، ولا تكون من الشجاج والكلام في الشجاج ولذا لم يذكر الخارصة والدامغة لأنها لا يبقى لها في الغالب أثر . وهذه الشجاج تختص بالرأس والوجه وما كان في غيرهما يسمى جراحة فهذا هو الحقيقة والحكم يترتب على الحقيقة فلا يجب بالجراحة ما يجب بالشجة من المقدار لأن التقدير بالنقل وهو إنما ورد في الشجاع وهي تختص بالرأس والوجه فخص الحكم المقدم بها، ولا يجوز إلحاق الجراحة بها دلالة ولا قياساً لأنها ليست في معناها في الشين لأن الوجه والرأس يظهران في الغالب وغيرهما مستور غالباً لا يظهر. واختلفوا في اللحيين فعندهما في الوجه فيتحقق الشجاج فيهما فيجب فيها موجبها خلافاً لما يقول مالك رحمه الله فإنه يقول إنهما ليسا من الوجه لأن المواجهة لا تقع بهما، ونحن نقول هما متصلان بالوجه من غير فاصل ويتحقق معنى المواجهة فصار كالذقن لأنهما تحتها. وقال شيخ الإسلام ويجب أن يفرض غسلهما في الوضوء لأنهما من الوجه حقيقة إلا أنا تركناهما للإجماع ولا إجماع هنا فبقينا العيرة للحقيقة .
The mutalahimah is that which cuts the flesh, and the difference found in the shijaj goes back to the basis of derivation, not to the ruling. As-samhaq is that which reaches the samhaq, which is the thin membrane between the flesh and the skull bone. Al-mawdihah is that which exposes the bone, i.e., makes it clear. Al-hashimah is that which breaks the bone. Al-munaqqilah is that which moves the bone after breakage, i.e., displaces it. Al-ammah is that which reaches the mother of the brain, and the mother of the brain is the thin membrane that gathers the brain. After the ammah is a wound called ad-damighah with the dotted ghain, which reaches the brain. Muhammad did not mention it because life does not remain after it usually, so it is killing, and it is not among the shijaj, and the discussion is about shijaj. For this reason he did not mention al-kharisah and ad-damighah because they usually leave no trace. These shijaj are specific to the head and face; what is elsewhere is called a wound (jirahah). This is the reality, and the ruling follows the reality. Thus, what is obligatory for a wound is not the same as what is obligatory for a shajjah in amount, because the estimation is by transmission, and it came only for shijaj, and they are specific to head and face, so the ruling is specified to them. It is not permissible to analogize wounds to them by indication or analogy because they are not similar in meaning in terms of shame, because the face and head are usually visible while others are usually covered and not visible. They differed about the jaws (lahyayni): according to him (Abu Hanifa's students) they are part of the face, so shijaj occur on them and their due is obligatory, contrary to what Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) says, for he says they are not part of the face because facing (muwajahah) does not occur with them. We say they are connected to the face without a separator and the meaning of facing is realized, so they are like the chin because they are under it. And Shaykh al-Islam said: It should be obligatory to wash them in wudu because they are truly part of the face, except that we omitted them due to consensus, but there is no consensus here, so we remain with the consideration of reality.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the types of head wounds (shijaj) and their legal specificity. The author explains that terms like mutalahimah, samhaq, mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, ammah, and damighah derive from linguistic roots and describe degrees of injury to the skull. Muhammad al-Shaybani omitted damighah because it is lethal and thus homicide, not a mere wound. The ruling for shijaj is restricted to head and face because the prophetic traditions (hadith) specify those; other body injuries are called jirahah and have different rules (hukumat or estimated compensation). Analogy is rejected because the head and face are normally exposed, unlike other parts. A significant intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute arises about the jaws (lahyayni): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider them part of the face, so shijaj rulings apply; Imam Malik excludes them because they are not prominent in facing. The Hanafi position relies on physical continuity with the face and the fact that they are beneath the chin, thus included. The author supports this by noting that jaws should logically be washed in wudu if not for consensus, but here no consensus exists.

Key Terms
شجة (shajjah): A wound specific to the head or face that exposes bone or reaches the brain.
جراحة (jirahah): A wound on any part of the body other than the head/face.
لحيان (lahyayni): The two jaws (mandibles).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because jirahah are more severe
B) Because the head and face are usually exposed, causing more shame
C) Because the hadith do not mention jirahah
D) Because of consensus among scholars

Key Takeaway
Shijaj are wounds of the head/face with fixed diyat; the Hanafi school includes the jaws as part of the face for these rulings.

The Eleven Types of Head Wounds (Shijaj) in Hanafi Fiqh
Enumeration and definition of head wound types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish each of the eleven types of head wounds based on their definitions, and explain the order of severity including the disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Head wounds are classified by depth into eleven types, each affecting diyat.

In Hanafi fiqh, head wounds (shijaj) are not all treated equally. The law distinguishes eleven specific types based on the depth of the wound, from a simple skin split to a fatal penetration of the brain. This classification directly influences the amount of blood money (diyah) or other legal consequences. Understanding these definitions is essential for anyone involved in qisas or diyat cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound's depth indicator
Read each definition carefully and note what layer of the head (skin, flesh, bone, brain covering, brain) is reached or affected.
The severity generally increases with depth.
2
Distinguish between similar types
Pay close attention to the differences between badi'ah and mutalahimah, and between hashimah and munaqqilah. Understand why the order matters.
The disagreement between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf affects the numbering.
3
Understand the linguistic roots
Each name derives from an Arabic verb (e.g., kharas, dama'a, bad'a). Connect the meaning to the wound's description.
This aids memory and precise application.
4
Note omissions by Imam Muhammad
Recognize that Imam Muhammad omitted kharisah, dami'ah, and damighah from his own works, and understand why (no trace, invariably fatal).
This affects the authoritative status of the count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المبسوط : الشجاج في الرأس والوجه أحد عشر أولها الخارصة وهي تشق الجلد مأخوذة من قولهم خرص القصار الثوب إذا شقه من الدق ثم الدامعة وهي التي يخرج منها ما يشبه الدمع مأخوذة من دمع العين. ولم يذكرها محمد لأنها لم يبق لها أثر في الغالب، ثم التي يخرج منها الدم ثم الباضعة وهي التي تبضع اللحم، ثم المتلاحمة، وعن محمد أنه جعل المتلاحمة قبل الباضعة خلافاً لأبي يوسف وتفسيرها عند أبي يوسف التي تقشر الجلد وتجمع اللحم في موضع الجراحة ولا تقطعه مأخوذة من التحام؛ يقال التحم الجيشان إذا اجتمعا . ثم السمحاق وهي التي تصل إلى جلدة رقيقة فوق العظم تسمى السمحاق، ثم الموضحة وهي التي توضح العظم واللحم، ثم الهاشمة وهي التي تهشم العظم، ثم المنقلة التي يخرج منها العظم لأنها تكسر العظم وتنقله عن موضعه، ثم الآمة التي تصل إلى أم الرأس وهي الجلدة التي فوق الدماغ، ثم الدامغة التي تخرق الجلد وتصل إلى الدماغ ولم يذكرها محمد لأن الإنسان لا يعيش معها.
And in al-Mabsut: The shijaj on the head and face are eleven. The first is al-kharisah: it splits the skin, derived from their saying 'the fuller split the garment when he beat it'. Then ad-dami'ah: that from which something like tears comes out, derived from 'tears of the eye'. Muhammad did not mention them because they usually leave no trace. Then that from which blood comes out. Then al-badi'ah: that which cuts the flesh. Then al-mutalahimah. And from Muhammad that he placed al-mutalahimah before al-badi'ah, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Its interpretation according to Abu Yusuf: it that peels the skin and gathers the flesh in the place of the wound without cutting it, derived from iltiham (joining); it is said 'the two armies joined when they came together'. Then as-samhaq: that which reaches a thin skin above the bone called samhaq. Then al-mawdihah: that which exposes the bone and flesh. Then al-hashimah: that which breaks the bone. Then al-munaqqilah: that from which bone comes out, because it breaks the bone and moves it from its place. Then al-ammah: that which reaches the mother of the head, which is the skin above the brain. Then ad-damighah: that which pierces the skin and reaches the brain, and Muhammad did not mention it because a person does not survive it.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from al-Mabsut (by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani) provides a comprehensive enumeration of eleven types of head wounds. Starting with the least severe: kharisah (cuts skin), dami'ah (produces fluid like tears), then the one that bleeds (no specific name given). Then badi'ah (cuts flesh), mutalahimah (flesh is crushed but not cut — note disagreement: Muhammad places it before badi'ah whereas Abu Yusuf places it after; the interpretation differs). Then samhaq (reaches the membrane above bone), mawdihah (exposes bone), hashimah (breaks bone), munaqqilah (bone is displaced), ammah (reaches the brain covering), and finally damighah (penetrates to brain, inevitably fatal). Imam Muhammad omitted kharisah, dami'ah, and damighah from his primary text (presumably al-Jami' al-Saghir or similar) because they usually leave no trace or are fatal, thus not falling under shijaj rulings.

Key Terms
شجاج (shijaj): Plural of shajjah; head wounds that reach the bone or skull.
السمحاق (samhaq): A wound that reaches the thin membrane (periosteum) covering the bone.
الموضحة (mawdihah): A wound that exposes the bone, clearing away the flesh.
الهاشمة (hashimah): A wound that breaks (crushes) the bone without displacement.
المنقلة (munaqqilah): A wound that breaks the bone and displaces it from its location.
الآمة (ammah): A wound that reaches the 'mother of the head' (umm al-ra's), the brain covering.
الدامغة (damighah): A wound that pierces the skin and reaches the brain itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hashimah breaks bone without displacement; munaqqilah moves the bone.
B) Hashimah exposes bone; munaqqilah only cuts flesh.
C) Munaqqilah is before hashimah in severity.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of head wounds into eleven types relies on precise anatomical definitions; the order includes an intra-madhab disagreement but the definitions themselves are consistent.

Fixed Diyat Amounts for Intentional Head Wounds
Stating fixed diyat amounts for intentional shijaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fixed diyat amounts for the five major intentional head wounds and identify which wounds have no fixed compensation.

Core Idea
Only major shijaj have fixed diyat; minor wounds require hukumat.

In Hanafi fiqh, intentional head wounds (shijaj) are classified into specific categories, each with a prescribed diyat amount. The passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq states the fixed amounts for mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, ammah, and ja'ifah, and clarifies that smaller wounds like kharisah have no fixed arsh. Understanding these categories is essential for proper application of diyat law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of shijaj
List the wound types mentioned: mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, ammah, ja'ifah, and minor ones (kharisah, dami'ah, damiyah).
The categories are based on severity and depth.
2
Define each wound type
Match each Arabic term to its description: mawdihah exposes bone, hashimah fractures bone, munaqqilah displaces bone, ammah reaches the brain membrane, ja'ifah penetrates the body cavity.
Ja'ifah is for torso; head wounds cannot be ja'ifah.
3
Extract the diyat fraction for each major type
From the passage, note the fraction of full diyah: mawdihah = half a tenth (1/20), hashimah = a tenth (1/10), munaqqilah = a tenth and a half (3/20), ammah = a third (1/3), ja'ifah = a third (1/3) if not penetrating, two-thirds (2/3) if penetrating.
Full diyah is 100 camels; multiply fraction to get camel count.
4
Note the exception for penetrating ja'ifah
If the ja'ifah penetrates through to the other side, the diyat increases from one-third to two-thirds.
This is unique to ja'ifah.
5
Recognize minor wounds with no fixed amount
The passage states that kharisah, dami'ah, and damiyah have no fixed arsh; they are subject to hukumat (estimated compensation).
Hukumat is based on the judge's estimation of the injury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أحكامها فإن كانت هذه الشجاج عمدا ففي الموضحة القصاص لأن السكين ينتهي إلى العظم ولا يخاف منه الهلاك غالبا فيجب القصاص لقوله تعالى والجروح قصاص [المائدة : ٥] وذكر الكرخي عنه أنه ليس في شيء من الموضحة نصف عشر الدية وفي الهاشمة عشرها وفي المنقلة عشر ونصف عشر وفي الآمة والجائفة ثلثها فإن نفذ من الجائفة فثلثاها وفي الخارصة والدامعة والدامية الشجاج إلا في القصاص والموضحة وليس لهذا الشجاج أروش مقدرة
As for their rulings: if these shijaj are intentional, then in al-mawdihah there is qisas because the knife reaches the bone and destruction is not usually feared, so qisas is obligatory due to His statement 'and wounds are qisas' [5:45]. Al-Karkhi mentioned from him (Abu Hanifa) that in nothing of the mawdihah is there half a tenth of the diyah, in al-hashimah a tenth, in al-munaqqilah a tenth and a half, in al-ammah and al-ja'ifah a third, and if it penetrates from the ja'ifah then two-thirds. And in al-kharisah, ad-dami'ah, ad-damiyah — shijaj except for qisas and mawdihah — and these shijaj have no fixed arsh.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings for intentional head wounds. For mawdihah (wound exposing bone), qisas (retaliation) is allowed because it reaches bone without typically causing death, based on Quran 5:45. However, al-Karkhi reports that Abu Hanifa says none of these have fixed amounts? The text here is somewhat ambiguous: it says 'ليس في شيء من الموضحة نصف عشر الدية' which seems contradictory — perhaps meaning that for mawdihah the amount is half a tenth (i.e., 5 camels = 1/20 of diyah? Actually standard is 5 camels = 1/20 = half a tenth? No: diyah is 100 camels, half a tenth is 5? Wait: half of a tenth is 5 camels, but that would be 1/20. The text says 'نصف عشر الدية' which is 5 camels. So it means: in mawdihah is half a tenth (5 camels), in hashimah a tenth (10 camels), in munaqqilah a tenth and a half (15 camels), in ammah and ja'ifah a third (33.33? Actually a third of full diyah = 33.33 camels, but 'ثلثها' means third of the diyah, so 33 and 1/3), and if the ja'ifah penetrates through, then two-thirds. The smaller wounds like kharisah, dami'ah, damiyah have no fixed amounts; they are subject to hukumat (estimated compensation).

Key Terms
موضحة (mawdihah): A wound that exposes the bone; qisas is applicable.
جائفة (ja'ifah): A wound that reaches the internal cavity (jawf) of the body.
هاشمة (hashimah): A wound that fractures the bone.
منقلة (munaqqilah): A wound that fractures and displaces the bone.
آمة (ammah): A wound that reaches the brain covering (membrane).
خارصة (kharisah): A scratch that removes skin but does not cause bleeding.
دامعة (dami'ah): A wound that bleeds but does not flow.
دامية (damiyah): A wound from which blood flows.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes fixed diyat for five major intentional head wounds, with the amount depending on severity, while minor wounds require estimated compensation.

Diyat for Accidental Head Wounds: Fixed Amounts and Prophetic Proof
Presentation of fixed diyat amounts for accidental head wounds with prophetic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fixed diyat amounts for mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, and ammah accidental wounds and identify the hadith evidence from Ali.

Core Idea
Fixed diyat amounts for head wounds are derived from a Prophetic tradition via Ali — no estimation for these categories.

In Hanafi fiqh, wounds (shijaj) have specific compensations. For accidental head wounds, the law specifies amounts for certain severities: mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, and ammah. This lesson presents those amounts and the Prophetic tradition that establishes them.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the types of shijaj
Identify the four categories mentioned: mawdihah (bone-exposing), hashimah (fracture), munaqqilah (displacing bone), and ammah (reaching brain).
These are specific to head wounds.
2
Memorize the fixed amounts
For each type, note the exact diyat as given: mawdihah = 5 camels, hashimah = 10, munaqqilah = 15, ammah = 1/3 of full diyah.
Pre-mawdihah uses hukumat 'adl (equitable estimation).
3
Examine the Prophetic evidence
The passage cites a letter from the Prophet to Hazm in Yemen, transmitted by Ali, listing diyat for various body parts, confirming these amounts.
The hadith is ahad (single narration) but clear in meaning.
4
Understand the 'aqilah clause
The text states that the obligation of these shijaj is not borne by the 'aqilah. This suggests that even in accidental cases, the offender pays personally, which is an exception to the general rule.
This point requires further investigation but is taken as stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وموجب هذه الشجاج لا يتحمله العاقلة، فإن كانت هذه الشجاج خطأ ففيما قبل الموضحة حكومة عدل لأنه ليس لها أرش مقدر وفي الموضحة خمس من الإبل وفي الهاشمة عشر من الإبل، وفي المنقلة خمسة عشرة، وفي الآمة ثلث الدية ؛ هكذا روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه كتب إلى حزم حين بعثه إلى اليمين وذكر فيه أن في النفس مائة من الإبل، وفي الأنف الدية، وفي الشفتين الدية، وفي اللسان الدية وفي العنين الدية وفي الصلب الدية وفي الذكر الدية، وفي الأنثيين الدية، وفي الرجل نصف الدية وفي الآمة ثلث الدية وفي الجائفة ثلث الدية، وفي ا المنقلة خمس عشرة من الإبل، وفي الموضحة خمس من الإبل؛ هكذا رواه علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه.
And the liability for these shijaj is not borne by the 'aqilah. If these shijaj are accidental, then for what is before mawdihah there is hukumat 'adl because there is no fixed arsh for it. And for mawdihah five camels, for hashimah ten camels, for munaqqilah fifteen, for ammah a third of the diyah. Thus it is narrated from the Prophet (PBUH) that he wrote to Hazm when he sent him to Yemen, mentioning therein that for life (nafs) one hundred camels, for the nose the full diyah, for the lips the full diyah, for the tongue the full diyah, for the 'anayn the full diyah, for the spine the full diyah, for the male organ the full diyah, for the testicles the full diyah, for a leg half the diyah, for ammah a third of the diyah, for ja'ifah a third of the diyah, for munaqqilah fifteen camels, for mawdihah five camels. Thus narrated by Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage states the rulings for accidental head wounds. The 'aqilah (tribal insurer) does not bear liability for these fixed amounts; they are on the offender's own property unless it is unintentional and then 'aqilah pays? Actually the text says 'لا يتحمله العاقلة' meaning the 'aqilah does not bear these? Wait: usually in accidental killing or wounds, the 'aqilah pays the diyat. But here it says the obligation of these shijaj is not borne by the 'aqilah? Possibly meaning for intentional? The context is about accidental? The sentence is ambiguous. Then it details: for wounds less severe than mawdihah, there is hukumat 'adl (equitable estimation) because no fixed amount. Mawdihah: 5 camels, hashimah: 10, munaqqilah: 15, ammah: 1/3 diyah (33.33). It then cites the famous prophetic letter to Hazm (or Abu Hazm?) sent to Yemen, as transmitted by Ali. This letter lists diyat for various body parts and confirms the amounts for mawdihah, munaqqilah, ammah, and ja'ifah. The full diyah for certain single organs is also mentioned.

Key Terms
شجاج (shijaj): Head wounds that break the skin. Plural of shajjah.
موضحة (mawdihah): A head wound that exposes the bone.
هاشمة (hashimah): A head wound that fractures the bone.
منقلة (munaqqilah): A head wound that displaces a bone fragment.
آمة (ammah): A head wound that reaches the brain (membrane).
جائفة (ja'ifah): A penetrating wound reaching the body cavity.
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): Equitable estimation of compensation by a judge when no fixed amount exists.
عاقلة ('aqilah): The paternal relatives (asabah) who assist in paying diyat for accidental killings/wounds.

Key Takeaway
The fixed diyat amounts for accidental head wounds (mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, ammah) are directly taken from a prophetic letter transmitted by Ali.

Qisas and Arsh for a Bald Man: Equality and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qisas and arsh for a bald victim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why qisas is not required when a bald man is wounded by a non-bald assailant, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation for medical expenses.

Core Idea
Equality in qisas is literal: no qisas if the victim's wound site is physically incomparable.

This lesson examines a rare transmission from Imam Muhammad regarding a bald man who receives an intentional wound (mawdihah). The core issue is whether qisas can be applied when the victim's scalp lacks hair, making the wound less comparable to that of a haired person. The passage also addresses the permissibility of consent to qisas and the dispute between Imam Muhammad and the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) on liability for medical expenses if no trace remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
Read the opening: a bald man is wounded intentionally by a non-bald person. Imam Muhammad rules no qisas but arsh. Why? Equality (musawat) is impossible because the nature of the wound differs on a bald scalp.
Analogy: a sound hand vs. a paralyzed hand (ashall) – qisas not applied.
2
Examine the consent exception
Even if the wounder consents to qisas, it is not allowed because the right to qisas did not arise. The injury does not necessitate qisas, so consent cannot create it.
Qisas is a fixed right, not a negotiable penalty.
3
Apply when both are bald
If both the wounder and victim are bald, qisas applies because equality is achievable. The text analogizes to two persons with paralyzed hands: qisas is allowed.
Equality is measured by the physical condition of both parties.
4
Analyze the dispute on expenses (no trace)
If the wound heals leaving no trace, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no compensation. Muhammad says the victim should be reimbursed for medical expenses incurred due to fear of the wound spreading (sarrayah). Understand each argument.
The dispute hinges on whether fear of sarrayah creates legal compulsion.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author sides with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: spending on treatment was voluntary because sarrayah is uncertain. Thus no liability.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النوادر : رجل أصلع ذهب شعره شجه إنسان موضحة عمداً قال محمد: لا يقتص وعليه الأرش لأنه أقل من موضحة لأن المساواة معتبرة في تناول الأطراف ولا مساواة لأن الموضحة في أحدهما مؤثرة في الجلد واللحم فتعذر مراعاة المساواة وصار كصحيح اليد إذا قطع يد الأشل لا يقطع فكذا هذا وإن قال الشاج رضيت أن يقتص مني ليس له ذلك لأن الجناية إذا لم توجب القصاص لا يوجب الاستيفاء بالرضا، وإن كان الشاج أيضاً أصلع عليه القصاص لأن اعتبار المساواة ممكن فصار كالأشل إذا قطع يد الأشل، وإن لم يبق للجراحة أثر فعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لا شيء عليه، وعند محمد يلزمه قدر ما أنفق عليه إلى أن يبرأ لأنه بجنايته اضطر إلى الإنفاق على الجراحة خوفاً من السراية فكان الزوال مضافاً إلى جنايته . لهما أنه كان مختاراً في الإنفاق ولم يكن مضطراً فيه لأن لحوق السراية لا يثبت الاضطرار لأن السراية موهومة فلا يثبت الاضطرار بالوهم والارتياب فلم يصر مفوتاً لشيء من المال ولا من المنفعة والجمال فلا يضمن كما لو لطمه فآلمه .
And in al-Nawadir: A bald man whose hair has gone, someone wounded him with an intentional mawdihah. Muhammad said: There is no qisas, and he must pay arsh because it is less than a mawdihah? Because equality is considered in taking limbs, and there is no equality because the mawdihah in one of them is effective on skin and flesh, so observance of equality is impossible, and it becomes like a sound hand when the hand of a person with a paralyzed hand (ashall) is cut: he is not cut. So likewise this. And if the wounder says 'I am pleased that qisas be taken from me', he does not have that right because if the injury does not necessitate qisas, the execution (of qisas) by consent is not obligatory. And if the wounder is also bald, then qisas is upon him because observance of equality is possible, so it becomes like an ashall when he cuts the hand of another ashall. And if no trace of the wound remains, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, there is nothing upon him. According to Muhammad, he must pay the amount he spent on it until healing, because due to his injury he was compelled to spend on the wound fearing sarrayah (spread of injury), so the removal (of trace) is attributed to his injury. Their (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argument: he was voluntary in spending, not compelled, because the occurrence of sarrayah does not establish compulsion, since sarrayah is imaginary, and compulsion by imagination and doubt is not established. So he did not cause loss of any wealth or benefit or beauty, so he does not guarantee, just as if he slapped him and caused pain.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage from al-Nawadir (rare transmissions) presents a case from Imam Muhammad. A bald man has a mawdihah inflicted intentionally. Muhammad rules no qisas because equality (musawat) is required for qisas in limbs and wounds. Since the victim's mawdihah is less severe (because no hair? Actually the text says 'أقل من موضحة' maybe meaning the effect is less damaging due to absence of hair? Or it means the wound itself is a mawdihah, but the lack of hair makes the effect less comparable? The reasoning: the mawdihah on a bald head affects skin and flesh more directly, but the scalp is different, so equality is not possible. This is analogized to a sound hand cutting the hand of a person with a paralyzed hand; qisas is not done. Even if the aggressor consents to qisas, it is not allowed because the right is not established. However, if both are bald, qisas applies because equality is possible. Additionally, if the wound heals leaving no trace, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no compensation (since no permanent damage), but Muhammad says the victim is entitled to reimbursement for medical expenses incurred due to the wound, because the fear of sarrayah (infection spreading) forced him to spend. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue that the spending was voluntary because sarrayah is uncertain (mawhum), so it does not create legal compulsion.

Key Terms
أصلع (asla'): A bald man, having no hair on the scalp.
أشل (ashall): A person with a paralyzed or unusable hand.
سراية (sarrayah): The potential spread of the wound leading to death or worsening.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what is due from the aggressor if the wound heals leaving no trace?
A) Nothing because no permanent damage.
B) The full diyah for mawdihah.
C) The amount the victim spent on treatment.
D) Qisas because the wound was intentional.

Key Takeaway
Equality in physical condition is a prerequisite for qisas; without it, only arsh is due, and the majority Hanafi view denies compensation for medical expenses if no trace remains.

Fixed Diyat Amounts and the Definition of Ja'ifah
Summarizing diyat amounts and clarifying the definition of ja'ifah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fixed diyat amounts for mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, ammah, and ja'ifah, and explain the Hanafi definition of ja'ifah including the dispute about its application to the head.

Core Idea
Ja'ifah reaches the internal cavity — whether trunk or head — and triggers a fixed diyat.

This passage restates the specific diyat amounts for five types of wounds: mawdihah, hashimah, munaqqilah, ammah, and ja'ifah. It then focuses on defining ja'ifah, a wound that penetrates to an internal cavity (jawf). The author notes a dispute: some restrict ja'ifah to the trunk, but the preferred Hanafi view includes head cavity wounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound types and their amounts
List the five wounds mentioned: mawdihah (half a tenth of diyah), hashimah (a tenth), munaqqilah (a tenth and a half), ammah and ja'ifah (a third each), and if ja'ifah penetrates through, two-thirds.
These amounts are fixed and based on transmitted evidence.
2
Parse the definition of ja'ifah
From the text, ja'ifah is defined as a wound that reaches the internal cavity (jawf) from the chest, abdomen, back, side, or neck as far as drink passes. Note that this definition from al-Idah initially implies trunk wounds only.
The author notes that mentioning ja'ifah in the section on shijaj (head wounds) may be accidental.
3
Analyze the dispute: does ja'ifah apply to the head?
Later scholars corrected the earlier view. They argued that ja'ifah can also occur in the head cavity (cranial cavity) if the wound penetrates the skull, because the head has a cavity. Thus, ja'ifah is not restricted to the trunk.
This adjustment integrates ja'ifah into the shijaj category when it occurs on the head.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The preferred (mufta bihi) position is that ja'ifah includes head cavity wounds. This is based on later Hanafi authorities (mutakhkhirin) who corrected the earlier restriction.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the correction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وفي الموضحة نصف عشر الدية وفي الهاشمة عشرها وفي المنقلة عشر ونصف عشر وفي الآمة والجائفة ثلثها فإن نفذ من الجائفة فثلثاها لما روي وقد قدمناه ولأنها إذا نفذت صارت جائفتين فيجب في كل واحدة منهما الثلث وهو يكون في الرأس والبطن. وقوله «جائفة» في الإيضاح : : الجائفة ما يصل إلى الجوف من الصدر والبطن والظهر والجنب وما وصل من الرقبة إلى الموضع الذي وصل إليه الشراب وما فوق ذلك فليس بجائفة. قال في النهاية ومعراج الدراية بعد نقل ذلك : فعلى هذا ذكر الجائفة هنا في مسائل الشجاج وقع اتفاقاً، وكذا في العناية نقلاً عن النهاية أقول : نعم على ما ذكر في الإيضاح يكون الأمر كذلك إلا أن غیره تدارکه قال فيما بعد وقالوا : الجائفة تختص بالجوف وجوف الرأس أو جوف البطن يعني أنها لما تناولت ما في جوف الرأس أيضاً كانت من الشجاع فيما إذا وقعت في الرأس فتدخل في
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And in mawdihah half a tenth of the diyah, in hashimah a tenth, in munaqqilah a tenth and a half, in ammah and ja'ifah a third, and if it penetrates from the ja'ifah then two-thirds, due to what has been narrated and we have already presented it, and because when it penetrates it becomes two ja'ifahs, so in each of them a third is wajib, and that occurs in the head and abdomen.' And his saying 'ja'ifah' in al-Idah: 'Al-ja'ifah is what reaches the internal cavity (jawf) from the chest, abdomen, back, side, and what reaches from the neck to the place where drink reaches, and what is above that is not ja'ifah.' He said in an-Nihayah and Miraj ad-Dirayah after quoting that: 'Thus upon this, mentioning ja'ifah here in the issues of shijaj occurred accidentally.' And similarly in al-Inayah quoting from an-Nihayah. I say: Yes, according to what is mentioned in al-Idah, the matter is so, except that someone else corrected it? He said later: 'And they said: ja'ifah is specific to the cavity, and the cavity of the head or the cavity of the abdomen, meaning that since it also includes what is in the cavity of the head, it is among the shijaj when it occurs in the head, so it enters...'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage re-states the fixed diyat amounts for intentional wounds (as previously given) and then discusses the definition of ja'ifah. The author clarifies that ja'ifah is a wound that reaches an internal cavity (jawf), which can be the chest, abdomen, back, side, or neck as far as drink passes. This definition from al-Idah implies that ja'ifah normally applies to trunk wounds, but the author notes that it is mentioned here in the section on shijaj (head wounds) seemingly by coincidence. However, some later scholars corrected this, stating that ja'ifah can also apply to the head cavity (cranial cavity) when the wound penetrates the skull, thus it does fit within shijaj. The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
جائفة (ja'ifah): A wound reaching the internal cavity, including head cavity according to the preferred view.
موضحة (mawdihah): A wound that exposes the bone (scalp wound).
هاشمة (hashimah): A wound that fractures the skull.
منقلة (munaqqilah): A skull fracture that requires shifting of bone pieces.
آمة (ammah): A head wound that reaches the brain covering (meninges).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correction mentioned in the passage, where can a ja'ifah wound occur?
A) Only in the abdomen
B) Only in the head
C) In both the head and abdomen cavities
D) Only in the chest

Key Takeaway
Ja'ifah is defined by penetration to any internal cavity (including head), and its fixed diyat is one-third (non-penetrating) or two-thirds (penetrating through).

Diyat for Head Wounds: Amounts and Prophetic Proof
States diyat amounts for types of head wounds and cites Prophetic hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyat amounts for each type of head wound (shijaj) and cite the Prophetic hadith that establishes them.

Core Idea
The Prophet fixed specific camel counts for each head wound: mawdihah 5, hashimah 10, munqilah 15, ammah and ja'ifah 1/3 diya.

Head wounds vary in severity, and Islamic law assigns distinct compensation amounts to each type. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists the diyat for mawdihah, hashimah, munqilah, ammah, and ja'ifah, and informs us that these rulings are based on a written instruction from the Prophet to Huzzam when he was sent to Yemen. Understanding these fixed amounts ensures fair compensation and reflects the precision of Hanafi fiqh in matters of injury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wound types and their rulings
Note the different categories of head wounds mentioned: mawdihah, hashimah, munqilah, ammah, ja'ifah, and the less severe ones (kharisah, dami'ah, etc.). For each, the passage states a specific diyat amount.
The amounts are given both as fractions of the full diya and in camel counts.
2
Locate the Prophetic dalil
The passage cites a hadith in which the Prophet wrote to Huzzam listing diyat amounts for various injuries, including the specific camel counts for head wounds. Another narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib confirms these numbers.
This is an ahad hadith, but its explicit numbers are considered decisive (dalala qati) in setting the quantities.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative statement listing exact camel counts (e.g., 'five camels') indicates that these amounts are wajib (obligatory) compensation, not negotiable or discretionary.
In Hanafi usul, such explicit numbers from a hadith override general principles of discretionary compensation.
4
Note the scope and exceptions
The passage also notes that for less severe wounds (below mawdihah), there is no fixed arsh; instead, a hukumat 'adl (equitable assessment) applies. It also distinguishes that these fixed amounts apply only outside retaliatory justice (qisas) and that the 'aqilah does not bear the obligation for these shijaj.
This clarifies the boundaries of the rule: only for accidental injuries (khatta) and not for intentional ones subject to qisas.
5
Confirm the Hanafi tarjih
The amounts listed are from zahir al-riwayah and are mufta bihi in the Hanafi school. While other schools may have minor variations, the Hanafi reliance on this hadith is clear.
The authority is strong: both the letter of the Prophet and the narration of Ali corroborate the same numbers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات
الموضحة نصف عشر الدية وفي الهاشمة عشرها وفي المنقلة عشر ونصف عشر وفي الآمة والجائفة ثلثها فإن نفذ من الجائفة فثلثاها وفي الخارصة والدامعة والدامية الشجاج إلا في القصاص والموضحة وليس لهذا الشجاج أروش مقدرة وموجب هذه الشجاج لا يتحمله العاقلة، فإن كانت هذه الشجاج خطأ ففيما قبل الموضحة حكومة عدل لأنه ليس لها أرش مقدر وفي الموضحة خمس من الإبل وفي الهاشمة عشر من الإبل، وفي المنقلة خمسة عشرة، وفي الآمة ثلث الدية ؛ هكذا روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه كتب إلى حزم حين بعثه إلى اليمين وذكر فيه أن في النفس مائة من الإبل، وفي الأنف الدية، وفي الشفتين الدية، وفي اللسان الدية وفي العنين الدية وفي الصلب الدية وفي الذكر الدية، وفي الأنثيين الدية، وفي الرجل نصف الدية وفي الآمة ثلث الدية وفي الجائفة ثلث الدية، وفي ا المنقلة خمس عشرة من الإبل، وفي الموضحة خمس من الإبل؛ هكذا رواه علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه.
The book of Diyat. Al-Mawdihah: half of a tenth of the diya. Al-Hashimah: its tenth. Al-Munqilah: a tenth and half a tenth. Al-Ammah and al-Ja'ifah: its third. If the ja'ifah penetrates, then its two-thirds. Al-Kharisah, al-Dami'ah, al-Damiyah, al-Badi'ah, al-Mutalahimah, al-Simhaq: (these) head wounds (shijaj), except in qisas and mawdihah; there are no fixed arsh for these shijaj, and the obligation for these shijaj is not borne by the 'aqilah. If these shijaj occur by mistake, then for what is before al-mawdihah, there is a hukumat 'adl because it has no fixed arsh. For al-mawdihah: five camels. For al-hashimah: ten camels. For al-munqilah: fifteen camels. For al-ammah: one third of the diya. This is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he wrote to Huzzam when he sent him to Yemen, mentioning in it: for the life: one hundred camels; for the nose: the full diya; for the lips: the full diya; for the tongue: the full diya; for the testicles: the full diya; for the back: the full diya; for the penis: the full diya; for the two testicles: the full diya; for the leg: half the diya; for the ammah: one third of the diya; for the ja'ifah: one third of the diya; for the munqilah: fifteen camels; for the mawdihah: five camels. Thus narrated Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states the diyat amounts for various head wounds (shijaj). It first summarizes: mawdihah (exposing bone) = 1/10 diya, hashimah (fracture) = 1/10, munqilah (displaced bone) = 1.5/10, ammah (reaching membrane) and ja'ifah (penetrating to cavity) = 1/3 each; if ja'ifah cuts through, it becomes 2/3. It then provides the Prophetic hadith via the letter to Huzzam and Ali's narration, specifying exact camel numbers. This establishes the rulings as based on revelation.

Key Terms
الموضحة (al-mawdihah): A head wound that exposes the bone.
الجائفة (al-ja'ifah): A wound penetrating to the body cavity (chest, abdomen, etc.).
الهاشمة (al-hashimah): A fracture of the skull bone.
المنقلة (al-munqilah): A wound that fractures and displaces the bone.
الآمة (al-ammah): A wound that reaches the membrane covering the brain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 1/10 of the diya
B) 5 camels
C) Both A and B
D) 10 camels

Key Takeaway
The Prophet fixed precise diyat amounts for specific head wounds, establishing a clear and just compensation system.

Qisas on a Bald Man: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Mawdihah
Discusses ruling on qisas and arsh for a deliberate mawdihah on a bald man

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on qisas for a mawdihah on a bald man and identify the thamarah al-khilaf regarding compensation for medical expenses.

Core Idea
Equality in qisas requires identical wounds; baldness disrupts equality, triggering different rulings on compensation.

This passage from al-Nawadir presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the issue of qisas and monetary compensation for a deliberate head wound (mawdihah) on a bald man. The core question is whether the impossibility of exact equality due to baldness negates qisas, and whether the victim's medical expenses incurred out of fear of infection are recoverable.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two elders) hold regarding qisas for a mawdihah on a bald man when the wounder is not bald. Also note their ruling when the wound heals without trace.
The two elders say no qisas because equality is impossible; if the wound disappears, no compensation is due.
2
Identify the opposing view within the school
Identify Muhammad al-Shaybani's position on the same two issues. He also says no qisas but requires arsh; for the disappearing wound, he demands compensation for medical expenses.
Muhammad applies a different reasoning based on the victim's compulsion due to fear of infection.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain Muhammad's dalil: the victim was forced to spend on treatment because of fear that the wound would spread (sara'yah), so the aggressor caused that loss.
He analogizes to cases where aggression directly forces expenditure.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain the rebuttal of the two elders: the victim's spending was voluntary, not compelled, because the fear of spread is merely hypothetical (mawhum). Without compulsion, the aggressor is not liable.
They argue that reliance on hypothetical harm does not establish legal compulsion.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference between the two positions: Muhammad holds the aggressor liable for medical costs if the wound heals; the two elders hold no liability.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the view of the two elders.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النوادر : رجل أصلع ذهب شعره شجه إنسان موضحة عمداً قال محمد: لا يقتص وعليه الأرش لأنه أقل من موضحة لأن المساواة معتبرة في تناول الأطراف ولا مساواة لأن الموضحة في أحدهما مؤثرة في الجلد واللحم فتعذر مراعاة المساواة وصار كصحيح اليد إذا قطع يد الأشل لا يقطع فكذا هذا وإن قال الشاج رضيت أن يقتص مني ليس له ذلك لأن الجناية إذا لم توجب القصاص لا يوجب الاستيفاء بالرضا، وإن كان الشاج أيضاً أصلع عليه القصاص لأن اعتبار المساواة ممكن فصار كالأشل إذا قطع يد الأشل، وإن لم يبق للجراحة أثر فعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لا شيء عليه، وعند محمد يلزمه قدر ما أنفق عليه إلى أن يبرأ لأنه بجنايته اضطر إلى الإنفاق على الجراحة خوفاً من السراية فكان الزوال مضافاً إلى جنايته . لهما أنه كان مختاراً في الإنفاق ولم يكن مضطراً فيه لأن لحوق السراية لا يثبت الاضطرار لأن السراية موهومة فلا يثبت الاضطرار بالوهم والارتياب فلم يصر مفوتاً لشيء من المال ولا من المنفعة والجمال فلا يضمن كما لو لطمه فآلمه .
In al-Nawadir: A bald man whose hair had gone, someone inflicted on him a deliberate mawdihah. Muhammad said: He is not subject to qisas, and upon him is the arsh, because it is less than mawdihah, because equality is considered in dealing with limbs, and there is no equality because the mawdihah in one of them affects the skin and flesh, thus maintaining equality is impossible. It becomes like a healthy hand when he cuts the hand of one who has a withered hand: no qisas. Similarly here. If the wounder also is bald, then qisas is upon him because it is possible to consider equality. It becomes like a withered hand when he cuts the hand of a withered hand. If no trace of the wound remains, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: nothing is due. According to Muhammad: he must pay the amount spent on treatment until recovery, because due to his aggression the victim was forced to spend on the wound for fear of spread (sara'yah); thus the disappearance (of the wound) is attributed to his aggression. Their (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's) argument: he was free in spending and not forced, because the occurrence of spread does not establish compulsion since spread is hypothetical; thus compulsion is not established by hypothesis and doubt. So he (the victim) did not lose any wealth, benefit, or beauty, so no compensation, just as if he slapped and it pained him.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage from al-Nawadir discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute about a deliberate mawdihah wound on a bald man. Muhammad says no qisas because equality cannot be ensured (skin and flesh vary), so arsh is due. If both are bald, qisas applies. For a wound that heals without trace, the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say no compensation, but Muhammad holds the aggressor must pay for medical costs incurred out of fear of infection. The elders argue that fear of spread is hypothetical and does not create compulsion. This ikhtilaf shows how different usul (principles) lead to different furu' (branches).

Key Terms
أصلع (asla'): A bald man whose hair has fallen out.
السراية (al-sarayah): The spread of infection or harm from the wound that could lead to death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, what is due from an aggressor who wounds a bald man's head (mawdihah) and the wound later heals without trace?
A) Nothing
B) The arsh (full diyat)
C) The amount spent by the victim on treatment
D) Qisas

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, the two elders' view that hypothetical harm does not create compulsion is the relied-upon position, meaning no compensation for a wound that disappears without trace.

Hukumat 'Adl for Minor Wounds: The Principle and its Calculation
Ruling on hukumat 'adl with intra-madhhab dispute on calculation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that minor wounds without fixed arsh require hukumat 'adl and explain the difference between al-Tahawi's valuation method and al-Karkhi's proportional method.

Core Idea
Minor wounds with no fixed arsh need fair compensation, but Hanafi scholars debate: by value decrease or by size proportion.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on wounds that have no prescribed diyat (arsh). The source text establishes that for six minor categories of shijaj, a 'hukumat 'adl' (fair assessment) is wajib. However, the method of calculation is contested among Hanafi authorities. You will learn the two main approaches and on which view the later fatwa relies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wounds without fixed arsh
From the source list: al-kharisah, al-dami'ah, al-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, al-simhaq. Confirm that for these, no specific amount is set in the Shari'ah.
These are all superficial head wounds below the level of mawdihah.
2
State the legal principle
Because these wounds cannot be disregarded, compensation is wajib via hukumat 'adl. This is supported by the practice of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz.
3
Examine al-Tahawi's method
Al-Tahawi: assess a slave's value without the wound, then with the wound. The difference in value relative to the total value is applied to the full diya. For example, if loss is 3% of value, then 3% of diya.
This method uses market price decrease.
4
Examine al-Karkhi's method
Al-Karkhi: measure the wound's size (e.g., length or area) relative to the size of a mawdihah (which has fixed arsh of half a tenth of diya). Then take that proportion of half a tenth.
Al-Karkhi argues that Tahawi's method could lead to absurd results (compensation exceeding mawdihah).
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
Later authorities like al-Marghinani and Shaykh al-Islam preferred al-Karkhi's method. Al-Sadr al-Shahid allowed flexibility: use Karkhi if possible in head/face wounds; otherwise use Tahawi's for ease.
The mufta bihi for head and face wounds is Karkhi's proportional method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وفي الموضحة نصف عشر الدية وفي الهاشمة عشرها وفي المنقلة عشر ونصف عشر وفي الآمة والجائفة ثلثها فإن نفذ من الجائفة فثلثاها لما روي وقد قدمناه ولأنها إذا نفذت صارت جائفتين فيجب في كل واحدة منهما الثلث وهو يكون في الرأس والبطن. وقوله «جائفة» في الإيضاح : : الجائفة ما يصل إلى الجوف من الصدر والبطن والظهر والجنب وما وصل من الرقبة إلى الموضع الذي وصل إليه الشراب وما فوق ذلك فليس بجائفة. قال في النهاية ومعراج الدراية بعد نقل ذلك : فعلى هذا ذكر الجائفة هنا في مسائل الشجاج وقع اتفاقاً، وكذا في العناية نقلاً عن النهاية أقول : نعم على ما ذكر في الإيضاح يكون الأمر كذلك إلا أن غیره تدارکه قال فيما بعد وقالوا : الجائفة تختص بالجوف وجوف الرأس أو جوف البطن يعني أنها لما تناولت ما في جوف الرأس أيضاً كانت من الشجاع فيما إذا وقعت في الرأس فتدخل في مسائل الشجاج باعتبار ذلك فلا يكون ذكرها في فصل الشجاج فيما وقع اتفاقاً بخلاف سائر الشجاج فإنه حيث لا يكون إلا في الرأس والوجه، وقيل لا تتحقق الجائفة فيما فوق الحلق . قال رحمه الله : (وفي الخارصة والدامعة والدامية والباضعة والمتلاحمة والسمحاق حكومة عدل) لأن هذه ليس فيها أرش مقدر من جهة الشرع ولا يمكن إهدارها فيجب فيها حكومة عدل، وهو مأثور عن ابراهيم النخعي وعمر بن عبد العزيز. واختلفوا في تفسير هذه الحكومة قال الطحاوي تفسيرها أن يقوم مملوكاً بدون هذا الأثر ثم يقوم وبه هذا الأثر ثم ينظر إلى تفاوت ما بينهما، فإن كان ثلث عشر القيمة مثلاً يجب ثلث عشر الدية، وإن كان ربع عشر القيمة يجب ربع عشر الدية. وقال الكرخي : ينظر كم مقدار هذه الشجة من الموضحة فيجب بقدر ذلك من نصف عشر الدية لأن ما لا نص فيه يرد إلى المنصوص عليه، وكان الكرخي رحمه الله يقول : ما ذكره الطحاوي ليس بصحيح لأنه اعتبر ذلك الطريق فربما يكون نقصان القيمة أكثر من نصف الدية فيؤدي إلى أن يوجب في هذه الشجاج وهو دون الموضحة أكثر مما أوجبه الشرع في الموضحة وأنه محال بل الصحيح الاعتبار بالمقدار. وقال الصدر الشهيد : ينظر المفتي في هذا إن أمكنه الفتوى بالثاني بأن كانت الجناية في الرأس والوجه يفتي بالثاني وإن لم يتيسر عليه ذلك يفتي بالقول الأول لأنه الأيسر قال؛ وكان المرغيناني يفتي به . وقال في المحيط : والأصح أنه ينظر كم مقدار هذه الشجة من أقل شجة لها أرش مقدر، فإن كان مقداره مثل نصف شجة لها أرش أو ثلثها وجب نصف أو ثلث أرش تلك الشجة، وأن ربعاً ،فربع ذكره بعد ذكر القولين فكأنه جعله قولاً ثالثاً والأشبه أن يكون هذا تفسيراً لقول الكرخي. وقال شيخ الإسلام وقول الكرخي أصح لأن علياً اعتبره بهذا الطريق فيمن قطع طرف لسانه على ما بيناه.
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (In al-mawdihah: half a tenth of the diya; in al-hashimah: its tenth; in al-munqilah: a tenth and half a tenth; in al-ammah and al-ja'ifah: its third; if the ja'ifah penetrates, then its two-thirds) because it has been narrated and we have mentioned it, and because when it penetrates, it becomes two ja'ifahs, so each one requires a third. And it occurs in the head and stomach. His saying 'ja'ifah' in al-Idah: al-ja'ifah is what reaches the cavity of the chest, stomach, back, and side, and what reaches from the neck to the place where drink reaches; above that is not ja'ifah. He said in al-Nihayah and Ma'arij al-Dirayah after that: According to this, the mention of ja'ifah here in the section of shijaj is coincidental. Similarly in al-'Inayah, quoting from al-Nihayah. I say: Yes, as mentioned in al-Idah, the matter is so, but others dealt with it and said: al-ja'ifah is specific to the cavity, and the cavity of the head or the cavity of the abdomen, meaning that if it affects what is in the head's cavity, it is also among the shijaj when it occurs in the head. So it enters into the section of shijaj in that respect, so its mention in the section of shijaj is not coincidental, unlike other shijaj which occur only in the head and face. It is said: al-ja'ifah is not realized above the throat. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (In al-kharisah, al-dami'ah, al-damiyah, al-badi'ah, al-mutalahimah, and al-simhaq: hukumat 'adl) because these have no fixed arsh from the Shari'ah, and they cannot be disregarded, so hukumat 'adl is obligatory. This is narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz. They differed in interpreting this hukumat. Al-Tahawi said its interpretation is that a slave is valued without this effect, then valued with this effect, and the difference between the two is seen. If it is, for example, a third of a tenth of the value, then a third of a tenth of the diya is obligatory; if a quarter of a tenth, then a quarter. Al-Karkhi said: See how much this shajjah is from al-mawdihah, and accordingly from half a tenth of the diya is obligatory, because what is not legislatively specified is referred to what is specified. Al-Karkhi used to say: What al-Tahawi mentioned is not correct, because he considered that method, and perhaps the decrease in value may be more than half the diya, leading him to impose on these shijaj (which are below mawdihah) more than what the Shari'ah imposed on mawdihah, which is absurd. Rather, the correct method is the consideration of measure. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The mufti considers: if he can give fatwa by the second (al-Karkhi's method) in head and face wounds, he does so; otherwise, he gives fatwa by the first (al-Tahawi's) as it is easier. Al-Marghinani used to give fatwa by it. In al-Muhit: The most correct is to see how much this shajjah is from the smallest shajjah with a fixed arsh. If it is equal to half or third of that shajjah, then half or third of the arsh of that shajjah is obligatory; if a quarter, then a quarter. He mentioned it after the two opinions, as if he made it a third opinion. It is more appropriate that this be an interpretation of al-Karkhi's statement. Shaykh al-Islam said: Al-Karkhi's statement is more correct, because 'Ali considered it by this path in the case of one who cut the tip of his tongue, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage returns to the matn, restating diyat amounts for mawdihah, etc. It then defines ja'ifah and discusses whether it includes head wounds. The major focus is on hukumat 'adl for minor wounds (kharisah etc.) that have no fixed arsh. The text presents three scholarly views: (1) Tahawi's method of measuring decrease in slave value, (2) Karkhi's method of measuring wound size relative to mawdihah, (3) Sadr al-Shahid's pragmatic approach. The author notes that al-Marghinani preferred Karkhi's method, which Shaykh al-Islam also considered strongest, citing the precedent of 'Ali regarding a tongue wound.

Key Terms
حكومة عدل (hukumat 'adl): A fair assessment of compensation determined by a judge, proportional to the injury, when no fixed arsh is prescribed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which method of calculating hukumat 'adl is considered more correct by Shaykh al-Islam?
A) Al-Tahawi's method of valuing a slave with and without the wound
B) Al-Karkhi's method of measuring the wound's size relative to mawdihah
C) Al-Sadr al-Shahid's method of using the smallest wound with fixed arsh
D) The method of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz

Key Takeaway
For wounds without fixed arsh, hukumat 'adl is wajib, and the preferred Hanafi method for head/face wounds is al-Karkhi's proportional size comparison to mawdihah.
